10.1016/j.jmp.2018.12.003
State-trace models differ from most models in mathematical psychology in that they are neither parametric models nor computational models. These models may be loosely described as consisting of a small number of “boxes and arrows”, plus accompanying assumptions. 
                      1 
                    
                   
                      1 
                      In this respect, state-trace models are like the models studied in Systems Factorial Technology ( Little, Altieri, Fifić, & Yang, 2017 ). 
                    What is it that state-trace models do? Suppose we know that two independent variables jointly affect each of two dependent variables. State-trace models propose that there is a “bottleneck” between the independent variables and the dependent variables. There is a  single  latent variable that mediates the  entire  effect of the independent variables on the dependent variables. Thus, when an experimenter manipulates the independent variables, the experimenter is indirectly manipulating the latent variable and it is the latent variable is doing the real work of affecting the dependent variables. 
                      2 
                    
                   
                      2 
                      Actually state-trace modeling can be a bit broader than this: See Figs. 1–6 of  Bamber (1979) .

Imagine, then, an observer who was shown the values of the two dependent variables in each condition of the experiment but who was kept ignorant of the fact that there were two independent variables that changed from condition to condition. To that observer, it would look as though the results of the experiment could be explained by assuming that only a single independent variable was changed across conditions.

Discrediting state-trace models.  A state-trace model may be regarded as a  family  of more specific models that involve more specific assumptions. When we empirically discredit a state-trace model, we have discredited every more specific model in the family. This is a powerful result; tinkering with assumptions will not save the model.

State-trace analysis is a collection of methods for putting state-trace models to the test; these methods have been developed and refined over a period of decades ( Bamber, 1979; Davis-Stober, Morey, Gretton, & Heathcote, 2016; Dunn & Kalish, 2018; Loftus, Oberg, & Dillon, 2004; Prince, Brown, & Heathcote, 2012 ). This paper describes a way of overcoming a problem that can arise when attempting to discredit a state-trace model.

Example: memory.  Consider a natural phenomenon in which two independent variables causally influence two dependent random variables. For example, consider the natural phenomenon of memory. In the laboratory, an experimenter might manipulate the amount of time given to the subject to study a list of words; the experimenter might also manipulate the retention interval between studying the list and being tested for recall and recognition of the words. In this context, study time and retention interval are two independent variables and recall performance and recognition performance are two dependent variables. Note that, unlike the independent variables that are under the control of the experimenter, the dependent variables are random variables.

Suppose that we do an experiment and across trials of that experiment, we set the independent variable  
                         I 
                         V 
                       to any value within a broad range, whereas we set the independent variable  
                         H 
                         L 
                       to one of only two values: a larger value that we denote  
                         hi

                         lo

                         3

                               hi

                               lo 
                            .

                         H

                       might vary over a broad range of values. But, in our experiment, we restrict it to the two values  
                         hi 
                       and  
                         lo 
                      .) On each trial, after setting the values of the independent variables, we observe the value of one or the other of the dependent variables.

In other words, on each trial of the experiment, the experimenter must make three choices:

In their paper on the design of state-trace experiments,  Prince et al. (2012)  referred to the set of possible first choices as the  trace factor , to the set of possible second choices as the  dimension factor , and to the set of possible third choices as the  state factor .

Consider the model depicted in  Fig. 1 . In this model, the independent variables have only an indirect effect on the dependent variables. Thus, the values of the independent variables

The ranges of the functions  
                                           
                                              
                                                 λ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 hi

                                              ⋅

                                           I 
                                           V 
                                        -values  
                                           u 
                                           < 
                                           v 
                                         such that  
                                           
                                              (5) 
                                              
                                                 
                                                    
                                                       λ

                                                    u 
                                                    ) 
                                                 
                                                 < 
                                                 
                                                    
                                                       λ 
                                                    
                                                    
                                                       lo 
                                                    
                                                 
                                                 
                                                    ( 
                                                    v 
                                                    )

The above assumptions are collectively called the  Monotonic State-Trace Model Assumptions .

In the original formulation of state-trace models ( Bamber, 1979 ), the Model-Based Monotonicity Assumptions were not a formal part of the model. Furthermore, state-trace models can be tested even if the model does not include the Model-Based Monotonicity Assumptions and those assumptions are, in fact, false. (See Section  3  for further discussion.)

However, the work of Loftus and his coauthors ( Loftus, 1978; Loftus et al., 2004 ) have shown both the plausibility and usefulness of monotonicity assumptions. As a result, monotonicity assumptions have often been included in state-trace models. In particular,  Dunn and Kalish (2018)  have emphasized the value of monotonicity assumptions in deriving testable predictions from state-trace models. Indeed,  Theorem 2  of this paper provides an illustration of how monotonicity assumptions lead to a testable prediction that could not be made without those assumptions.

In this section, we define what a state trace is. We begin by defining certain useful functions.

Note that the definition of the above state traces does not require the assumptions of  Definition 1  to be correct.

Significant parts of this paper may be regarded as a mathematical formalization and elaboration of the ideas of  Prince et al. (2012) .  Theorem 1  falls into this category.

Repeatedly Used Fact.  In the proofs of  Theorems 1 and 3 , the following fact is repeatedly used: Suppose that the function

Checking for state-trace coincidence.  As  Prince et al. (2012)  have emphasized, in an experiment testing a state-trace model, it is important that the range of observed  
                         
                            
                               x 
                            
                            
                               lo 
                            
                         
                      -values should overlap the range of observed  
                         
                            
                               x 
                            
                            
                               hi 
                            
                         
                      -values. If the observed ranges of  
                         
                            
                               x 
                            
                            
                               lo 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               x 
                            
                            
                               hi 
                            
                         
                       do not overlap, then the experiment’s achievement is meager: It has not found any evidence that the model is wrong. On the other hand, it has failed to find persuasive evidence that the model is correct. 
                         5 
                       
                      
                         5 
                         See  Prince et al. ’s Figure 3 for illustrations supporting their argument.

It can be convenient to include monotonicity assumptions in state-trace models. Such assumptions make it easier to prove theorems about state-trace models and to empirically test such models. Thus, monotonicity assumptions were included in  Definition 1  and those assumptions were used in the proof of  Theorem 1 . However, such assumptions are not essential either to state-trace models or to state-trace analysis.

                                  lo 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               I 
                               V 
                               ) 
                            
                          for every  
                            I 
                            V 
                            ∈ 
                            
                               
                                  D 
                               
                               
                                  I 
                                  V 
                               
                            
                          a yellow stripe is painted on the ground. The set of points on the ground painted yellow is the state trace  
                            
                               
                                  ST 
                               
                               
                                  lo 
                               
                            
                         .

If the ranges of the functions  
                            
                               
                                  λ 
                               
                               
                                  hi 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  λ 
                               
                               
                                  lo 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          overlap, then (analogous to  Theorem 1 ) the red stripe and the yellow stripe will coincide (i.e., be identical over at least part of their lengths).

The key point.  If we want to test the hypothesis that the  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               hi 
                               ) 
                            
                          train and the  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               lo 
                               ) 
                            
                          train run on the same track (as opposed to the two-track hypothesis), we check whether the red stripe and the yellow stripe coincide over at least part of their lengths. (Of course, this method of testing the one-track hypothesis is not foolproof; it might happen that the two trains run on different tracks that happen to coincide for part of their lengths.)

Monotonicity assumptions.  Such assumptions are not essential in the evaluation of whether the red stripe and the yellow stripe partially coincide.

Consider, first, the assumption that  
                            
                               
                                  λ 
                               
                               
                                  hi 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          is monotonic increasing. If that function is not monotonic, then the  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               hi 
                               ) 
                            
                          train sometimes moves forward along the track (i.e., toward higher values of  
                            L 
                            V 
                         ) and sometimes backward. Because all we care about is the red stripe left by the  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               hi 
                               ) 
                            
                          train, then we do not care whether the train always moved in the same direction when it laid down the red stripe or whether it went back and forth. The same consideration applies to  
                            
                               
                                  λ 
                               
                               
                                  lo 
                               
                            
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                         .

Next, consider the assumption that the functions  
                            ξ 
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          and  
                            ι 
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          are both monotonic increasing. Recall that the route of the track is given by Eq.  (32) . Thus the functions  
                            ξ 
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          and  
                            ι 
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          specify the route of the railroad track.

Example: The track that always goes in more-or-less the same direction.  If  
                            ξ 
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          and  
                            ι 
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          are both monotonic increasing, then the route of the track will run between the southwest and the northeast.

Example: The track with one sharp bend.  Suppose, however, that  
                            ξ 
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          first increases and then decreases, whereas  
                            ι 
                            
                               ( 
                               ⋅ 
                               ) 
                            
                          always increases. Suppose further that a train moves along the track from lower values of  
                            L 
                            V 
                          to higher. The train will initially move in a northeastward direction and then, following the track, it will turn and move in a northwestward direction. Let us call the segment of the track where the train moves northwestward the  upper limb  of the track. And let us call the segment where the train moves northeastward the  lower limb .

What we are concerned with is whether the red stripe and the yellow stripe coincide over at least a part of their lengths. If we can verify that they do, then the route of the track does not matter to us. We conclude that the  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               hi 
                               ) 
                            
                          train and the  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               lo 
                               ) 
                            
                          train run on the same track, regardless of what its route is.

These assumptions ensure that the track’s shape is simple. As mentioned earlier, these assumptions imply that the track runs between the southwest and the northeast. Thus, given two distinct points on the track, one of them must be to the northeast of the other. So, if we observe a yellow point to the southeast of a red point, those two points cannot lie on the same track. (This issue is discussed at greater length in Section  4 .)

If  
                               ξ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  ⋅ 
                                  ) 
                               
                             and  
                               ι 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  ⋅ 
                                  ) 
                               
                             are not monotonic increasing, then the route of the railroad track is more complicated and testing the one-track hypothesis vs. the two-track hypothesis requires more care. Consider the example of the track with one sharp bend. Suppose that the one-track hypothesis is correct, but that we are unable to observe the entirety of either the red stripe or the yellow stripe. If the observed points on the red stripe are all on the upper limb of the track and the observed points on the yellow stripe are all on the lower limb of the track, then it will look as though the  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  H 
                                  L 
                                  = 
                                  hi 
                                  ) 
                               
                             train and the  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  H 
                                  L 
                                  = 
                                  lo 
                                  ) 
                               
                             are on different tracks.

Thus, if one assumes monotonicity of  
                               ξ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  ⋅ 
                                  ) 
                               
                             and  
                               ι 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  ⋅ 
                                  ) 
                               
                             when it is not true, state-trace analysis can lead to an incorrect conclusion. This potential for error is the essence of one of the critiques that  Ashby (2014)  made of state-trace analysis. 
                               7 
                             
                            
                               7 
                               This paper takes no position on Ashby’s other critique that state-trace analysis should not be used to assess the number of cognitive systems. 
                             See further comments by  Dunn and Kalish (2018, Section 2.3) .

If the track route becomes a bit more complicated, e.g., a track with two sharp bends, it becomes more difficult, but not impossible, to test the one-track hypothesis. However, if the track has a sufficiently pathologic shape, such as the non-periodic Lissajous curve discussed in  Bamber and van Santen (1985, Appendix A) , then it is impossible to test the one-track hypothesis if there is any error, no matter how small, in measuring the positions of points on the red and yellow stripes.

In this paper, those assumptions are used merely as technical assumptions. Specifically, they are used, together with the assumptions that  
                               ξ 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  ⋅ 
                                  ) 
                               
                             and  
                               ι 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  ⋅ 
                                  ) 
                               
                             are continuous and monotonic increasing, to derive that  
                               
                                  
                                     x 
                                  
                                  
                                     hi 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  ⋅ 
                                  ) 
                               
                            ,  
                               
                                  
                                     y 
                                  
                                  
                                     hi 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  ⋅ 
                                  ) 
                               
                            ,  
                               
                                  
                                     x 
                                  
                                  
                                     lo 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  ⋅ 
                                  ) 
                               
                            , and  
                               
                                  
                                     y 
                                  
                                  
                                     lo 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  ⋅ 
                                  ) 
                               
                             are continuous and monotonic increasing and, thus, to justify applying the Repeatedly Used Fact to those functions.

Consider the following assumptions from  Definition 1 :

First. 
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        ξ

                                           ⋅

ξ

                                           ⋅

                                        ι 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           ⋅ 
                                           )

λ

                                           ( 
                                           ⋅

                                           ⋅

The first assumption is used in the proofs of both  Theorem 1  and  Theorem 2 . But the second assumption is used only in the proof of  Theorem 1 .

Neither of the above assumptions is used in the proofs of  Theorems 3 and 4 . Instead those model-based assumptions are replaced in the proofs of  Theorems 3 and 4  with model-free monotonicity assumptions that are stated in  Definition 7  .

Monotonicity assumptions are not essential to state-trace analysis. But, when they are appropriate, they do make it easier to carry out a state-trace analysis. For further comments on the usefulness of monotonicity assumptions, see  Dunn and Kalish (2018, Chapter 2) .

The following theorem uses the Model-Based Monotonicity Assumptions to derive a testable prediction from state-trace models. This prediction or some similar prediction has been used by various investigators in testing state trace models—see  Dunn and Kalish (2018) . The idea expressed by the theorem is nothing new. But, whether the theorem has been formally stated before, I do not know.

Suppose that we are studying a particular natural phenomenon, which will be referred to here as the “phenomenon of interest”. Suppose further that we have thought about a monotonic state-trace model ( Definition 1 ) for that phenomenon and, in advance of experimentation, we think that the state-trace model is implausible. Thus, we have come to the tentative opinion that the monotonic state-trace model is most likely incorrect. How can we find empirical evidence that a monotonic state-trace model for the phenomenon of interest is wrong?

If we can achieve the following goal, then  Theorem 2  tells us that the state-trace model is wrong.

Goal. 
                         
                            Find a pair of delta-discordant points, with one point on the state trace for  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     H 
                                     L 
                                     = 
                                     hi 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                and the other point on the state trace for  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     H 
                                     L 
                                     = 
                                     lo 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               .

Find a pair of delta-discordant points, with one point on the state trace for  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     H 
                                     L 
                                     = 
                                     hi 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                and the other point on the state trace for  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     H 
                                     L 
                                     = 
                                     lo 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               .

Is the above goal too strong?  To demonstrate that the monotonic state-trace model is wrong, it is not necessary to find a pair of delta-discordant points;  Theorem 2  implies that it suffices to find a pair of points that are  not  delta-concordant, i.e., a pair of points that is either delta-discordant or delta-borderline. However, to empirically establish that two points  
                         
                            ( 
                            x 
                            , 
                            y 
                            ) 
                         
                       and  
                         
                            ( 
                            
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                            , 
                            
                               
                                  y 
                               
                               
                                  ′ 
                               
                            
                            ) 
                         
                       on state traces are delta-borderline is not feasible. It would be necessary to empirically demonstrate that either  
                         Δ 
                         x 
                         = 
                         
                            
                               x 
                            
                            
                               ′ 
                            
                         
                         − 
                         x 
                       is  exactly  zero or  
                         Δ 
                         y 
                         = 
                         
                            
                               y 
                            
                            
                               ′ 
                            
                         
                         − 
                         y 
                       is  exactly  zero. But, if there is any measurement error, it is not possible to demonstrate that a quantity is  exactly  zero rather than merely close to zero. For that reason, the above goal requires that a pair of delta-discordant points be found, rather than merely a pair of points that are not delta-concordant. 
                         8 
                       
                      
                         8 
                         Analogous concerns arise in signed-difference analysis ( Dunn & Anderson, 2018  p. 49) and over the question of whether ANOVA-style interactions can be “transformed away”: ( Loftus, 1978  pp. 314–315) and ( Wagenmakers, Krypotos, Criss, & Iverson, 2012  p. 151).

One delta-discordant pair is enough.  We only have to find one pair of delta-discordant points to demonstrate that a monotonic state-trace model is wrong. No matter how many delta-concordant pairs we observe, finding just one delta-discordant pair is enough to show the model wrong.

One approach to achieving the goal of finding a delta-discordant point-pair is to select two sets of

This is a reasonable approach to testing a state-trace model and sophisticated statistical methods have been developed for use with this approach—see  Dunn and Kalish (2018), Kalish, Dunn, Burdakov, and Sysoev (2016) .

If the state-trace model is wrong, then one hopes that the  
                         I 
                         V 
                      -values chosen in  (38)  will cause the points  (39)  and  (40)  to fall on the state traces in a manner similar to  Fig. 2 . That is, in such a way that there are a number of delta-discordances between the points  (39)  and the points  (40) .

However, if one has been unlucky in one’s choice of  
                         I 
                         V 
                      -values in  (38) , then the points  (39)  and  (40)  may fall on the state traces in a manner resembling  Fig. 3 , creating the illusion that the points all lie on a single state trace rather than on two spatially separated state traces. In other words, in such a way that there are no delta-discordances between the points  (39)  and the points  (40) .

Whether the results from the experiment look more like  Fig. 2  or more like the misleading results in  Fig. 3  depends on the experimenter’s luck in choosing the  
                         I 
                         V 
                      -values  (38) .

We will now describe a method that, as long as the state traces are spatially separated (rather than crossing each other), does not depend on luck in the choice of  
                         I 
                         V 
                      -values  (38)  for demonstrating the falsity of a state-trace model.

As before, suppose that we are interested in a particular natural phenomenon and have considered a monotonic state-trace model for that phenomenon. After some thought, we have arrived at the tentative opinion that the model is incorrect. We will attempt to show that the model is wrong by finding a clearcut violation of the prediction in  Theorem 2 . Thus, we will attempt to achieve the goal stated above in Section  5  of finding a pair of delta-discordant points, with one point on the state trace for  
                         
                            ( 
                            H 
                            L 
                            = 
                            hi 
                            ) 
                         
                       and the other point on the state trace for  
                         
                            ( 
                            H 
                            L 
                            = 
                            lo 
                            ) 
                         
                      .

We will pursue our goal as follows. First, we will arbitrarily select an

According to  Theorem 2 , if a state-trace model is correct, it should be impossible to find a discordance-generating  
                         b 
                      . So, if we find one, we know that no monotonic state-trace model of the natural phenomenon of interest can be correct.

We intend to use a method for finding a discordance-generating  
                            b 
                          that does not depend on luck. We will be guided by  Theorem 4  which tells us which  
                            b 
                         -values are discordance-generating, if any.

To prove  Theorem 4 , we will need to make some assumptions that guarantee that the state traces  
                            
                               
                                  ST 
                               
                               
                                  hi 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  ST 
                               
                               
                                  lo 
                               
                            
                          are well behaved. But what should those assumptions be? Recall that the purpose of  Theorem 4  is to provide a way of finding discordance-generating  
                            b 
                         -values when a monotonic state-trace model is wrong. Therefore, in proving  Theorem 4 , we cannot employ the Monotonic State-Trace Model Assumptions ( Definition 1 ) as that would amount to assuming that such models are correct. On the other hand, our new assumptions must be compatible with the Monotonic State-Trace Model Assumptions as, otherwise, our new assumptions would be tantamount to assuming that monotonic state-trace models are wrong when they might, in fact, be correct. We want a method of testing these models that is based on assumptions that are open-minded. To that end, we adopt the following assumptions that will be shown to be weaker than the Monotonic State-Trace Model Assumptions.

                               .  This case is illustrated in  Fig. 4 . Consider any

                                  b

                                        (49)

                                     ] 
                                  
                                are delta-discordant.

Case 3.

                                        hor

                                     (53)

                                                    ver 
                                                 
                                              
                                              )

                                lies on the state trace for

                                     H 
                                     L 
                                     = 
                                     lo 
                                     ) 
                                  
                                is delta-concordant with every other point on that state trace. Thus, if

                                     H 
                                     L 
                                     = 
                                     lo 
                                     )

                                     ] 
                                  
                                because the latter point also lies on the state trace for

                                     H 
                                     L 
                                     = 
                                     lo 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               . Therefore, there is no  
                                  b 
                                that is discordance generating.  □

To obtain a discordance-generating  
                            b 
                         ,  Theorem 4  shows us that we should choose a  
                            b 
                          that lies strictly between  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  ver 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  hor 
                               
                            
                         . The difficulty is that we do not know what  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  ver 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  hor 
                               
                            
                          are. But, as discussed in Sections  7  and  8 , there are ways to estimate them.

If the state trace for  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               lo 
                               ) 
                            
                          lies below and to the right of the state trace for  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               hi 
                               ) 
                            
                          then, as illustrated in  Fig. 4 ,  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  ver 
                               
                            
                          will be less than  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  hor 
                               
                            
                         . Then  Theorem 4  shows that discordance-generating  
                            b 
                         
 exist and indicates where to find them. In that case, we do not need luck to find a discordance-generating  
                            b 
                         . Conversely, if the state trace for  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               lo 
                               ) 
                            
                          lies above and to the left of the state trace for  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               hi 
                               ) 
                            
                         , then  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  ver 
                               
                            
                          will be greater than  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  hor 
                               
                            
                         . We do not need luck in that case either. But, in the special case where the two state traces cross each other 
                            11 
                          
                         
                            11 
                            The two state traces can cross each other even though the Model-Free Monotonicity Assumptions are satisfied. An example:

                                  I 
                                  V 
                                and  
                                  H

                                restricted to positive values and with  
                                  0 
                                  < 
                                  lo 
                                  < 
                                  hi 
                               . 
                          
                         at the point  
                         
                            
                               [ 
                               
                               
                                  
                                     x 
                                  
                                  
                                     hi 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  a 
                                  ) 
                               
                               , 
                               
                               
                                  
                                     y 
                                  
                                  
                                     hi

                                  a 
                                  ) 
                               
                               
                               ] 
                            
                         , then  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  ver 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  hor 
                               
                            
                          will be equal. In that case, because we have had the bad luck to unwittingly choose  
                            a 
                          such that  
                            
                               [ 
                               
                               
                                  
                                     x 
                                  
                                  
                                     hi 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  a 
                                  )

                                     y 
                                  
                                  
                                     hi 
                                  
                               
                               
                                  ( 
                                  a 
                                  ) 
                               
                               
                               ] 
                            
                          lies at the crossing point of the two state traces, then discordance-generating  
                            b 
                          will not exist. However, with other choices of  
                            a 
                         , discordance-generating  
                            b 
                          would exist.

Recall that we wish to find a discordance-generating  
                         b 
                      .  Theorem 4  tells us that any  
                         b 
                       that lies strictly between  
                         
                            
                               b 
                            
                            
                               ver 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               b 
                            
                            
                               hor 
                            
                         
                       is discordance generating. So, to find a discordance-generating  
                         b 
                      , we need to estimate  
                         
                            
                               b 
                            
                            
                               ver 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               b 
                            
                            
                               hor

                               b 
                            
                            
                               ver 
                            
                         
                       and  
                         
                            
                               b 
                            
                            
                               hor 
                            
                         
                      ; Section  8  describes another.

Hans Strasburger has maintained a web-based list of software libraries for psychophysics, including estimation of psychometric functions:  http://visionscience.com/documents/strasburger/strasburger.html

Families of psychometric functions.  Typically, psychometric functions are modeled as being members of a two-, three-, or four-parameter family of monotonic-increasing functions. Psychometric functions are estimated statistically by estimating their parameters. In other words, suppose that, for example, the psychometric function is modeled as belonging to a two-parameter family of functions that are denoted

Adaptive vs. non-adaptive methods of estimation.  There are two basic categories of methods for gathering data to estimate psychometric functions. On each trial of an experiment,  adaptive  methods decide what value to set the independent variable to on the basis of observations from earlier trials;  non-adaptive  methods do not do that.

To be more specific: There is to be a sequence of trials, Trial 1, Trial 2, etc., on which data is collected. On each Trial  
                                  i 
                               , we choose a value  
                                  
                                     
                                        v 
                                     
                                     
                                        i 
                                     
                                  
                                of the independent variable and then make an empirical observation of the value of the dependent random variable  
                                  D 
                                  
                                     
                                        V 
                                     
                                     
                                        X 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     ( 
                                     
                                        
                                           v 
                                        
                                        
                                           i 
                                        
                                     
                                     , 
                                     lo 
                                     ) 
                                  
                               . We denote that value by  
                                  
                                     
                                        w 
                                     
                                     
                                        i 
                                     
                                  
                               . Suppose that  
                                  m 
                                trials have been run so far. At this point, the data in hand consists of the ordered pairs  
                                  
                                     (59) 
                                     
                                        
                                           { 
                                           
                                           
                                              [ 
                                              
                                              
                                                 
                                                    v 
                                                 
                                                 
                                                    1 
                                                 
                                              
                                              ,

                                     m 
                                     + 
                                     1 
                                     )

                                        +

                                of the independent variable. Adaptive methods use the data collected so far  including the

                                     m 
                                     + 
                                     1 
                                     )

A bewildering variety of adaptive methods for estimation of psychometric functions have been developed. Reviews of adaptive methods have been written by  Treutwein (1995)  and  Leek (2001) . The latter review appeared in a special issue (Vol. 63, No. 8) of the journal  Perception & Psychophysics  that was devoted to psychometric functions and adaptive methods.

A more recent paper describing an adaptive method of psychometric-function estimation has been written by  Shen, Dai, and Richards (2015) .

Bayesian approach.  
                               Kuss, Jäkel, and Wichmann (2005)  have described a Bayesian method for psychometric-function estimation.

Overcoming failure of the usual assumptions.  A number of investigators have developed estimators of psychometric functions that are designed to work well even if some assumption usually employed in psychometric-function estimation is false.

• 
                                     When estimating psychometric functions, the psychometric function is typically modeled as belonging to a known parametric family of functions. Because  Foster and Żychaluk (2009)  were concerned that such a model assumption could be false, they developed a model-free method of estimating a psychometric function. 
                                  
                                  
                                     • 
                                     
                                        Fründ, Haenel, and Wichmann (2011)  developed an estimation method to cope with the possibility that the subject’s behavior might change over time. 
                                  
                                  
                                     • 
                                     
                                        Schütt, Harmeling, Macke, and Wichmann (2016)  developed an estimation method to cope with the possibility that the subject’s behavior might not be independent across trials.

When estimating psychometric functions, the psychometric function is typically modeled as belonging to a known parametric family of functions. Because  Foster and Żychaluk (2009)  were concerned that such a model assumption could be false, they developed a model-free method of estimating a psychometric function.

Fründ, Haenel, and Wichmann (2011)  developed an estimation method to cope with the possibility that the subject’s behavior might change over time.

Schütt, Harmeling, Macke, and Wichmann (2016)  developed an estimation method to cope with the possibility that the subject’s behavior might not be independent across trials.

Where do matters stand at this point? Recall that we are interested in a particular natural phenomenon and have considered a monotonic state-trace model for that phenomenon. After thought, we have come to the tentative opinion that the contemplated state-trace model is incorrect. We wish to do an experiment that we hope will yield data supporting our opinion.

From  Theorem 2 , we know that the state-trace model is wrong if there exists a point on the state trace for  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               lo 
                               )

                               hi 
                               )

We want to find such a pair of delta-discordant points (if they exist). So, we select a value  
                            a 
                          of the independent variable  
                            I 
                            V

                            b 
                          of  
                            I 
                            V

The complication is that the data collection is a three-step process: First, collect some data. Second, on the basis of the collected data, choose a value for  
                               
                                  
                                     b 
                                  
                                  
                                     can

                                     can

Here we discuss how practical is the approach to state-trace analysis described in this paper.

The methods proposed in this paper for discrediting monotonic state-trace models are based upon  Theorem 4 , which is itself based on  Theorem 3 . These theorems assume:

                               • 
                               The assumption that  
                                     a 
                                   is sufficiently small ( Definition 8 , Eq.  (44) ).

A reviewer suggested that the approach to testing state-trace models described in this paper may be less efficient than the methods already in the literature. That may be so. But those methods are vulnerable to bad luck as illustrated in  Fig. 3 . Such vulnerability to bad luck may be mostly eliminated by using closely spaced values of  
                            I 
                            V 
                         . But that is inefficient.

The methods described in the paper for testing state-trace models assume that the independent variable  
                            I 
                            V 
                          is continuous. However, many of the independent variables used in cognitive psychology are discrete. For example, in memory studies, a commonly used independent variable is number of study trials. If there are only small differences between adjacent values of the independent variable, then methods that assume continuity of the independent variable may perhaps be successfully adapted.

But what if the differences between adjacent values are large? One way of coping with this problem might be to modify the experiment. For example, if the independent variable is number of study trials and each study trial is four seconds in duration, the duration of study trials might be changed to one second.

Alternatively, if modifying the experiment is not feasible, one could apply any of the methods of state-trace analysis already in the literature. But, if adjacent  
                            I 
                            V 
                         -values are far apart, then adjacent points on the state traces will be far apart and there will be the danger of bad luck illustrated earlier in  Fig. 3 .

Monotonic state-trace models were defined ( Definition 1 ). It was shown that a monotonic state-trace model predicts that the state trace for  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               hi 
                               ) 
                            
                          and the state trace for  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               lo 
                               ) 
                            
                          will coincide ( Theorem 1 ). It was argued by means of a railroad-track analogy (Section  3 ) that monotonicity assumptions are not essential for state-trace analysis, but that such assumptions do make it easier to test state-trace models.

Suppose that we have formulated a monotonic state-trace model for a natural phenomenon of interest. However, after thought, the state-trace model seems implausible and we wish to find empirical evidence that the model is wrong. How can we do that?

Theorem 2  implies that the model is wrong if there exist two points, one on the state trace for  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               hi 
                               ) 
                            
                          and one on the state trace for  
                            
                               ( 
                               H 
                               L 
                               = 
                               lo 
                               ) 
                            
                         , that are delta-discordant. One method for finding two such points is to observe multiple points on each of the two state traces with the hope that, among those points, there will be a delta-discordant pair. But this approach can have bad luck and fail to find delta-discordant pairs even though they exist. An approach that is much less dependent on luck for finding a delta-discordant pair of points is formulated in this paper.

To be specific:  Theorem 3  defined the  
                            I 
                            V 
                          values  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  ver 
                               
                            
                          and  
                            
                               
                                  b 
                               
                               
                                  hor 
                               
                            
                          and  Theorem 4  showed that, if
